Demystifying the Chinese Housing Boom
Hanming Fang, Quanlin Gu, Wei Xiong, and Li-An Zhou
NBER Working Paper No. 21112
April 2015
JEL No. R3

                                            ABSTRACT

We construct housing price indices for 120 major cities in China in 2003-2013 based on sequential
sales of new homes within the same housing developments. By using these indices and detailed information
on mortgage borrowers across these cities, we find enormous housing price appreciation during the
decade, which was accompanied by equally impressive growth in household income, except in a few
first-tier cities. While bottom-income mortgage borrowers endured severe financial burdens by using
price-to-income ratios over eight to buy homes, their participation in the housing market remained
steady and their mortgage loans were protected by down payments commonly in excess of 35 percent.
As such, the housing market is unlikely to trigger an imminent financial crisis in China, even though
it may crash with a sudden stop in the Chinese economy and act as an amplifier of the initial shock.

       Such	 Â high	 Â income	 Â growth	 Â expectations	 Â might	 Â have	 Â resulted	 Â from	 Â extrapolative	 Â behavior	 Â as	 Â

demonstrate	 Â that	 Â regression	 Â to	 Â the	 Â mean	 Â is	 Â the	 Â single	 Â most	 Â robust	 Â and	 Â empirically	 Â relevant	 Â fact	 Â 
about	 Â cross-Â­â€country	 Â growth	 Â rates.	 Â Thus,	 Â they	 Â argue	 Â that	 Â while	 Â China	 Â might	 Â continue	 Â to	 Â grow	 Â for	 Â

       Frictions	 Â in	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â financial	 Â system	 Â might	 Â also	 Â have	 Â contributed	 Â to	 Â the	 Â high	 Â housing	 Â

                                                                                                                                                         4	 Â

       From	 Â an	 Â investment	 Â perspective,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â interesting	 Â to	 Â note	 Â a	 Â tale	 Â of	 Â two	 Â markets	 Â at	 Â the	 Â time	 Â of	 Â

       There	 Â are	 Â divergent	 Â views	 Â about	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â boom.	 Â Chow	 Â and	 Â Niu	 Â (2014)	 Â use	 Â a	 Â

                                                                                                                                                            5	 Â 
	 Â 
households	 Â to	 Â buy	 Â homes	 Â by	 Â undertaking	 Â substantial	 Â financial	 Â burdens,	 Â causing	 Â them	 Â to	 Â be	 Â

                             This	 Â paper	 Â is	 Â organized	 Â as	 Â follows.	 Â Section	 Â 1	 Â briefly	 Â describes	 Â some	 Â institutional	 Â background.	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    1. Institutional	 Â Background	 Â 

                             The	 Â development	 Â of	 Â housing	 Â markets	 Â in	 Â mainland	 Â China	 Â is	 Â a	 Â relatively	 Â new	 Â phenomenon.	 Â

                             Comprehensive	 Â housing	 Â reform	 Â was	 Â initiated	 Â in	 Â 1994	 Â when	 Â employees	 Â in	 Â the	 Â state	 Â sector	 Â

1
 	 Â Under	 Â current	 Â law,	 Â land	 Â used	 Â to	 Â build	 Â residential	 Â properties	 Â is	 Â leased	 Â for	 Â a	 Â term	 Â of	 Â 70	 Â years;	 Â after	 Â the	 Â expiration	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         6	 Â 
	 Â 
some	 Â large	 Â cities	 Â in	 Â early	 Â 1990s;	 Â but	 Â they	 Â grew	 Â rapidly	 Â only	 Â after	 Â 1998	 Â when	 Â the	 Â central	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  	 Â 
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    [Figure	 Â 1	 Â about	 Â Here]

                             These	 Â policies	 Â were	 Â effective	 Â in	 Â stimulating	 Â both	 Â the	 Â demand	 Â and	 Â supply	 Â of	 Â residential	 Â

18	 Â percent	 Â of	 Â annual	 Â growth.	 Â Figure	 Â 1	 Â provides	 Â a	 Â rough	 Â estimate	 Â of	 Â the	 Â supply	 Â of	 Â newly	 Â

                             It	 Â is	 Â common	 Â in	 Â China	 Â to	 Â separate	 Â cities	 Â into	 Â three	 Â tiers.	 Â The	 Â first	 Â tier	 Â includes	 Â the	 Â four	 Â cities	 Â

Tianjing	 Â and	 Â Chongqing	 Â (the	 Â two	 Â autonomous	 Â municipalities	 Â other	 Â than	 Â Beijing	 Â and	 Â Shanghai)	 Â 



	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
2
       	 Â See	 Â Appendix	 Â A	 Â for	 Â detailed	 Â information	 Â about	 Â mortgage	 Â loans	 Â in	 Â China.	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         7	 Â 
	 Â 
and	 Â capital	 Â cities	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 24	 Â provinces3	 Â and	 Â 9	 Â other	 Â cities,	 Â which	 Â are	 Â typically	 Â vital	 Â industrial	 Â or	 Â

                             The	 Â construction	 Â boom	 Â of	 Â residential	 Â housing	 Â in	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â started	 Â in	 Â the	 Â late	 Â 1990s,	 Â

From	 Â 2005,	 Â the	 Â new	 Â construction	 Â in	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â had	 Â slowed	 Â down	 Â substantially	 Â due	 Â to	 Â the	 Â

percent	 Â of	 Â total	 Â fixed	 Â asset	 Â investment	 Â and	 Â contributed	 Â to	 Â roughly	 Â one-Â­â€sixth	 Â of	 Â Chinaâ€™s	 Â GDP	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  	 Â 
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    [Figure	 Â 2	 Â about	 Â Here]

                             The	 Â development	 Â of	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â was	 Â also	 Â accompanied	 Â by	 Â an	 Â urbanization	 Â process	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                 2. Constructing	 Â a	 Â Chinese	 Â Housing	 Â Price	 Â Index	 Â 

                             To	 Â systematically	 Â examine	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â boom,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â important	 Â to	 Â construct	 Â an	 Â

	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
       	 Â Lasha,	 Â the	 Â capital	 Â of	 Â Tibet,	 Â is	 Â typically	 Â excluded	 Â from	 Â the	 Â list	 Â due	 Â to	 Â its	 Â special	 Â economic	 Â status.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
3



                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         8	 Â 
	 Â 
price	 Â index	 Â arises	 Â because	 Â a	 Â good	 Â price	 Â index	 Â requires	 Â that	 Â we	 Â compare	 Â the	 Â prices	 Â of	 Â the	 Â same	 Â 
(or	 Â at	 Â least	 Â comparable)	 Â houses	 Â over	 Â time.	 Â To	 Â the	 Â extent	 Â that	 Â the	 Â set	 Â of	 Â homes	 Â involved	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 
transactions	 Â in	 Â different	 Â periods	 Â of	 Â time	 Â is	 Â likely	 Â to	 Â be	 Â different,	 Â a	 Â price	 Â index	 Â constructed	 Â by	 Â 
simply	 Â comparing	 Â the	 Â mean	 Â or	 Â median	 Â sale	 Â prices	 Â per	 Â square	 Â meter	 Â likely	 Â measures	 Â not	 Â only	 Â 
the	 Â changes	 Â in	 Â the	 Â prices	 Â of	 Â similar	 Â homes,	 Â but	 Â also	 Â the	 Â changes	 Â in	 Â the	 Â composition	 Â of	 Â 
transacted	 Â homes.	 Â This	 Â problem	 Â is	 Â likely	 Â to	 Â be	 Â more	 Â severe	 Â in	 Â emerging	 Â housing	 Â markets	 Â than	 Â in	 Â 
mature	 Â ones	 Â because	 Â in	 Â emerging	 Â housing	 Â markets,	 Â homes	 Â in	 Â more	 Â central	 Â locations	 Â are	 Â likely	 Â 
to	 Â be	 Â built	 Â and	 Â transacted	 Â earlier	 Â than	 Â homes	 Â in	 Â city	 Â outer-Â­â€rings.	 Â 

A. Standard	 Â Methodologies	 Â 

       There	 Â are	 Â two	 Â standard	 Â methodologies	 Â that	 Â are	 Â widely	 Â used	 Â to	 Â construct	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â 
indices.	 Â These	 Â methods,	 Â which	 Â we	 Â review	 Â briefly	 Â below,	 Â are	 Â aimed	 Â at	 Â finding	 Â a	 Â suitable	 Â way	 Â to	 Â 
compare	 Â the	 Â prices	 Â of	 Â similar	 Â homes.	 Â 

       One	 Â prominent	 Â approach	 Â for	 Â constructing	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â is	 Â to	 Â use	 Â hedonic	 Â price	 Â regressions,	 Â 
which	 Â goes	 Â back	 Â to	 Â Kain	 Â and	 Â Quigley	 Â (1970).	 Â In	 Â this	 Â approach,	 Â the	 Â sales	 Â price	 Â is	 Â regressed	 Â on	 Â a	 Â 
set	 Â of	 Â variables	 Â that	 Â characterize	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â unit	 Â -Â­â€-Â­â€number	 Â of	 Â rooms,	 Â square	 Â feet	 Â of	 Â interior	 Â 
space,	 Â lot	 Â size,	 Â quality	 Â of	 Â construction,	 Â condition,	 Â and	 Â so	 Â forth.	 Â The	 Â regression	 Â coefficients	 Â can	 Â 
be	 Â interpreted	 Â as	 Â prices	 Â for	 Â implicit	 Â attributes.	 Â This	 Â hedonic	 Â approach	 Â can	 Â then	 Â be	 Â used	 Â to	 Â 
construct	 Â a	 Â price	 Â index	 Â in	 Â two	 Â ways	 Â (Case	 Â and	 Â Shiller,	 Â 1987).	 Â The	 Â first	 Â way	 Â to	 Â construct	 Â a	 Â price	 Â 
index	 Â is	 Â to	 Â run	 Â separate	 Â regressions	 Â on	 Â data	 Â from	 Â each	 Â time	 Â period.	 Â The	 Â estimated	 Â equations	 Â 
are	 Â then	 Â used	 Â to	 Â predict	 Â the	 Â value	 Â of	 Â a	 Â standard	 Â unit	 Â in	 Â each	 Â period,	 Â which	 Â is	 Â in	 Â turn	 Â used	 Â to	 Â 
construct	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â index	 Â for	 Â the	 Â standard	 Â unit.	 Â A	 Â second	 Â way	 Â is	 Â to	 Â run	 Â a	 Â single	 Â 
regression	 Â on	 Â the	 Â pooled	 Â data	 Â from	 Â sales	 Â in	 Â all	 Â time	 Â periods.	 Â Inclusion	 Â of	 Â a	 Â time	 Â dummy	 Â for	 Â the	 Â 
period	 Â of	 Â the	 Â sale	 Â allows	 Â the	 Â constant	 Â term	 Â to	 Â shift	 Â over	 Â time,	 Â reflecting	 Â movement	 Â in	 Â prices,	 Â 
again	 Â controlling	 Â for	 Â characteristics.	 Â 

       Whether	 Â hedonic	 Â price	 Â regressions	 Â can	 Â accurately	 Â capture	 Â price	 Â movements	 Â crucially	 Â 
depends	 Â on	 Â how	 Â well	 Â the	 Â data	 Â capture	 Â the	 Â actual	 Â characteristics	 Â and	 Â quality	 Â of	 Â the	 Â unit.	 Â 
Unobserved	 Â and	 Â time	 Â varying	 Â characteristics	 Â that	 Â are	 Â valued	 Â by	 Â the	 Â market	 Â but	 Â not	 Â captured	 Â in	 Â 
the	 Â data	 Â can	 Â lead	 Â to	 Â biased	 Â estimates	 Â of	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â index.	 Â This	 Â is	 Â a	 Â particular	 Â issue	 Â in	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                              9	 Â 
	 Â 
China.	 Â Due	 Â to	 Â the	 Â rapid	 Â expansion	 Â of	 Â Chinese	 Â cities,	 Â new	 Â housing	 Â units	 Â have	 Â been	 Â constructed	 Â 
mostly	 Â on	 Â land	 Â near	 Â the	 Â urban	 Â fringes.	 Â According	 Â to	 Â the	 Â China	 Â Urban	 Â Statistical	 Â Yearbook	 Â 
(published	 Â by	 Â Ministry	 Â of	 Â Housing	 Â and	 Â Urban-Â­â€Rural	 Â Development),	 Â the	 Â total	 Â size	 Â of	 Â developed	 Â 
urban	 Â area	 Â at	 Â the	 Â national	 Â level	 Â increased	 Â from	 Â 19,844	 Â square	 Â kilometers	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â (Form	 Â 3-Â­â€9	 Â on	 Â 
page	 Â 107)	 Â to	 Â 34,867	 Â square	 Â kilometers	 Â in	 Â 2013	 Â (Form	 Â 2-Â­â€12	 Â on	 Â page	 Â 90).	 Â Such	 Â a	 Â dramatic	 Â 
expansion	 Â of	 Â urban	 Â residential	 Â land	 Â parcels	 Â implies	 Â that	 Â unobserved	 Â time	 Â varying	 Â characteristics	 Â 
as	 Â transacted	 Â homes	 Â move	 Â from	 Â locations	 Â closer	 Â to	 Â city	 Â center	 Â to	 Â locations	 Â in	 Â city	 Â fringe	 Â is	 Â likely	 Â 
to	 Â lead	 Â to	 Â biased	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices.	 Â 

                             Case	 Â and	 Â Shiller	 Â (1987)	 Â popularized	 Â another	 Â method	 Â using	 Â repeated	 Â sales.	 Â 	 Â This	 Â approach	 Â 
originated	 Â with	 Â Baily,	 Â Muth,	 Â and	 Â Nourse	 Â (1963),	 Â who	 Â initially	 Â proposed	 Â a	 Â method	 Â involving	 Â a	 Â 
regression	 Â where	 Â the	 Â ğ‘– -Â­â€th	 Â observation	 Â of	 Â the	 Â dependent	 Â variable	 Â is	 Â the	 Â log	 Â of	 Â the	 Â price	 Â of	 Â the	 Â ğ‘– -Â­â€
th	 Â house	 Â at	 Â its	 Â second	 Â sale	 Â date	 Â minus	 Â the	 Â log	 Â of	 Â its	 Â price	 Â on	 Â its	 Â first	 Â sale	 Â date.	 Â The	 Â independent	 Â 
variables	 Â consist	 Â of	 Â only	 Â dummy	 Â variables,	 Â one	 Â for	 Â each	 Â time	 Â period	 Â in	 Â the	 Â sample	 Â except	 Â for	 Â 
the	 Â first	 Â (the	 Â base	 Â period	 Â for	 Â the	 Â index).4	 Â The	 Â estimated	 Â coefficients	 Â are	 Â then	 Â taken	 Â as	 Â the	 Â log	 Â 
price	 Â index.	 Â 	 Â This	 Â initial	 Â method	 Â builds	 Â on	 Â a	 Â strong	 Â assumption	 Â that	 Â the	 Â variance	 Â of	 Â the	 Â error	 Â 
term	 Â is	 Â constant	 Â across	 Â houses.	 Â 	 Â As	 Â this	 Â variance	 Â is	 Â likely	 Â to	 Â depend	 Â on	 Â the	 Â time	 Â interval	 Â 
between	 Â sales,	 Â Case	 Â and	 Â Shiller	 Â (1987)	 Â proposed	 Â a	 Â weighted-Â­â€repeated-Â­â€sales	 Â method	 Â with	 Â a	 Â two-Â­â€
step	 Â procedure	 Â to	 Â relax	 Â this	 Â assumption.5	 Â 	 Â 

                             The	 Â repeat	 Â sales	 Â approach	 Â does	 Â not	 Â require	 Â the	 Â measurement	 Â of	 Â quality;	 Â it	 Â only	 Â requires	 Â 
that	 Â the	 Â quality	 Â of	 Â individual	 Â units	 Â in	 Â the	 Â sample	 Â remains	 Â constant	 Â over	 Â time.	 Â However,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â well	 Â 
recognized	 Â that	 Â this	 Â repeated	 Â sales	 Â method	 Â wastes	 Â a	 Â large	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â transactions	 Â data	 Â because	 Â 
repeated	 Â sales	 Â may	 Â contribute	 Â to	 Â only	 Â a	 Â small	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â all	 Â housing	 Â transactions.	 Â More	 Â 
important,	 Â the	 Â set	 Â of	 Â homes	 Â that	 Â are	 Â sold	 Â repeatedly	 Â may	 Â not	 Â be	 Â representative	 Â of	 Â the	 Â general	 Â 
population	 Â of	 Â homes	 Â (see	 Â Mark	 Â and	 Â Goldberg,	 Â 1984).	 Â 
	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
4
  	 Â Specifically,	 Â for	 Â each	 Â house,	 Â the	 Â dummy	 Â variables	 Â are	 Â zero	 Â except	 Â for	 Â the	 Â dummy	 Â corresponding	 Â to	 Â the	 Â second	 Â 
sale	 Â (where	 Â it	 Â is	 Â +1)	 Â and	 Â for	 Â the	 Â dummy	 Â corresponding	 Â to	 Â the	 Â first	 Â sale	 Â (where	 Â it	 Â is	 Â -Â­â€1).	 Â If	 Â the	 Â first	 Â sale	 Â was	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 
first	 Â period,	 Â there	 Â is	 Â no	 Â dummy	 Â variable	 Â corresponding	 Â to	 Â the	 Â first	 Â sale.	 Â 
5
  	 Â In	 Â the	 Â first	 Â stage,	 Â they	 Â implement	 Â the	 Â Baily,	 Â Muth,	 Â and	 Â Nourse	 Â (1963)	 Â procedure	 Â and	 Â calculate	 Â the	 Â vector	 Â of	 Â 
regression	 Â residuals,	 Â which	 Â is	 Â then	 Â used	 Â to	 Â construct	 Â the	 Â weights	 Â to	 Â be	 Â used	 Â in	 Â the	 Â stage	 Â two	 Â regression.	 Â In	 Â the	 Â 
second	 Â step,	 Â a	 Â generalized	 Â least	 Â squares	 Â regression	 Â (with	 Â weights	 Â constructed	 Â from	 Â the	 Â regression	 Â residuals	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 
first	 Â stage)	 Â is	 Â run.	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    10	 Â 
	 Â 
B. A	 Â Hybrid	 Â Approach	 Â for	 Â Chinese	 Â Housing	 Â Markets	 Â 

                             We	 Â propose	 Â a	 Â hybrid	 Â approach	 Â of	 Â constructing	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â for	 Â a	 Â large	 Â number	 Â of	 Â

typically,	 Â apartments	 Â in	 Â development	 Â projects.6	 Â As	 Â a	 Â developer	 Â sells	 Â apartments	 Â in	 Â a	 Â project	 Â

 	 Â Our	 Â method	 Â of	 Â constructing	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â is	 Â related	 Â to	 Â the	 Â pseudo-Â­â€repeated	 Â sales	 Â price	 Â index	 Â recently	 Â

	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  3. The	 Â Chinese	 Â Housing	 Â Market	 Â Boom	 Â

 	 Â This	 Â assumption	 Â in	 Â principle	 Â can	 Â be	 Â relaxed.	 Â If	 Â each	 Â month	 Â we	 Â have	 Â a	 Â sufficiently	 Â large	 Â number	 Â of	 Â sales	 Â located	 Â in	 Â

indices.	 Â As	 Â a	 Â robustness	 Â check,	 Â we	 Â have	 Â divided	 Â Beijing	 Â into	 Â inner	 Â region	 Â that	 Â is	 Â close	 Â to	 Â the	 Â town	 Â center	 Â and	 Â 
suburban	 Â area	 Â and	 Â constructed	 Â separate	 Â indices	 Â for	 Â the	 Â two	 Â areas.	 Â The	 Â monthly	 Â changes	 Â of	 Â these	 Â two	 Â indices	 Â are	 Â 
highly	 Â correlated	 Â with	 Â a	 Â correlation	 Â of	 Â 0.93,	 Â although	 Â the	 Â index	 Â for	 Â the	 Â inner	 Â region	 Â grew	 Â slightly	 Â faster	 Â than	 Â that	 Â 
for	 Â the	 Â suburban	 Â area	 Â during	 Â our	 Â sample	 Â period.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
10
     	 Â 	 Â It	 Â is	 Â not	 Â clear	 Â how	 Â developers	 Â would	 Â change	 Â their	 Â pricing	 Â strategies	 Â during	 Â the	 Â course	 Â of	 Â sales	 Â of	 Â units	 Â in	 Â a	 Â 
project.	 Â Wu,	 Â Deng,	 Â and	 Â Liu	 Â (2014)	 Â provide	 Â some	 Â evidence	 Â from	 Â hedonic	 Â regressions	 Â that	 Â the	 Â unit	 Â prices	 Â tend	 Â to	 Â

months.	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       13	 Â 
	 Â 
one	 Â million	 Â mortgage	 Â loan	 Â contracts	 Â dating	 Â from	 Â the	 Â first	 Â quarter	 Â of	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â the	 Â first	 Â quarter	 Â of	 Â 
2013.	 Â A	 Â typical	 Â mortgage	 Â contract	 Â contains	 Â detailed	 Â information	 Â on	 Â the	 Â personal	 Â characteristics	 Â 
of	 Â home	 Â buyers	 Â (e.g.,	 Â age,	 Â gender,	 Â marital	 Â status,	 Â income,	 Â work	 Â unit,	 Â education,	 Â occupation,	 Â

(e.g.,	 Â complex	 Â location,	 Â floor	 Â level,	 Â and	 Â room	 Â number),	 Â as	 Â well	 Â as	 Â loan-Â­â€level	 Â characteristics	 Â (e.g.,	 Â 
maturity	 Â and	 Â down-Â­â€payment).	 Â

        Based	 Â on	 Â the	 Â transacted	 Â home	 Â prices	 Â and	 Â characteristics,	 Â we	 Â build	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â for	 Â

our	 Â sample	 Â period.	 Â Figure	 Â 4	 Â also	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â the	 Â bank	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â stayed	 Â in	 Â a	 Â narrow	 Â range	 Â 
between	 Â 2	 Â and	 Â 4	 Â percent	 Â during	 Â this	 Â period.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

        Our	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â allow	 Â us	 Â to	 Â precisely	 Â characterize	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â boom	 Â in	 Â the	 Â

A. First-Â­â€Tier	 Â Cities	 Â

        Figure	 Â 5	 Â depicts	 Â the	 Â monthly	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â for	 Â the	 Â four	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â in	 Â four	 Â

                                                                                                                                                            14	 Â

of	 Â downward	 Â movement.	 Â The	 Â first	 Â episode	 Â started	 Â in	 Â May	 Â 2008,	 Â when	 Â the	 Â price	 Â index	 Â was	 Â at	 Â

       As	 Â benchmarks	 Â for	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â appreciation,	 Â Panel	 Â A	 Â also	 Â plots	 Â two	 Â measures	 Â of	 Â the	 Â

       Panel	 Â B	 Â plots	 Â the	 Â monthly	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â index	 Â for	 Â Shanghai.	 Â The	 Â index	 Â increased	 Â from	 Â 1	 Â in	 Â

       The	 Â growth	 Â of	 Â householdsâ€™	 Â disposable	 Â income	 Â in	 Â Shanghai	 Â during	 Â this	 Â period	 Â was	 Â about	 Â the	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                     15	 Â

       Panels	 Â C	 Â and	 Â D	 Â respectively	 Â plot	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â for	 Â Guangzhou	 Â and	 Â Shenzhen.	 Â The	 Â

       Panels	 Â C	 Â and	 Â D	 Â also	 Â reveal	 Â that	 Â the	 Â per	 Â capita	 Â disposable	 Â income	 Â in	 Â Guangzhou	 Â nearly	 Â

January	 Â 2003	 Â through	 Â March	 Â 2013,	 Â as	 Â well	 Â as	 Â for	 Â sub-Â­â€periods	 Â from	 Â January	 Â 2003	 Â through	 Â

       To	 Â aggregate	 Â the	 Â price	 Â indices	 Â for	 Â the	 Â four	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â we	 Â construct	 Â a	 Â price	 Â index	 Â for	 Â the	 Â

month.	 Â The	 Â resulting	 Â index	 Â level	 Â represents	 Â the	 Â value	 Â of	 Â a	 Â housing	 Â portfolio	 Â constructed	 Â from	 Â

       Among	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â Panel	 Â A	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â the	 Â nominal	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â index	 Â had	 Â an	 Â average	 Â

       Panel	 Â A	 Â also	 Â reports	 Â the	 Â nominal	 Â growth	 Â of	 Â two	 Â measures	 Â of	 Â â€œpurchasing	 Â powerâ€:	 Â per	 Â capita	 Â

                             NBS	 Â Average	 Â Price	 Â Index.	 Â NBS	 Â also	 Â publishes	 Â the	 Â total	 Â floor	 Â area	 Â and	 Â revenue	 Â of	 Â houses	 Â sold	 Â

paid	 Â by	 Â total	 Â floor	 Â area	 Â of	 Â the	 Â transacted	 Â units	 Â in	 Â a	 Â given	 Â month	 Â and	 Â given	 Â city.	 Â 	 Â 

                             As	 Â pointed	 Â out	 Â in	 Â Deng,	 Â Gyourko,	 Â and	 Â Wu	 Â (2014b),	 Â both	 Â official	 Â series	 Â have	 Â well-Â­â€known	 Â 
issues	 Â and	 Â have	 Â been	 Â widely	 Â criticized:	 Â the	 Â NBS	 Â 70-Â­â€City	 Â Index	 Â is	 Â remarkably	 Â smooth	 Â and	 Â shows	 Â 
very	 Â little	 Â real	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â growth	 Â in	 Â 70	 Â Chinese	 Â cities	 Â in	 Â the	 Â last	 Â decade,	 Â while	 Â the	 Â NBS	 Â 
Average	 Â Price	 Â Index	 Â fails	 Â to	 Â control	 Â for	 Â quality,	 Â as	 Â it	 Â does	 Â not	 Â account	 Â for	 Â the	 Â fact	 Â that	 Â the	 Â newly	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    [Figure	 Â 8	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

                             Figure	 Â 8	 Â depicts	 Â our	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â index	 Â against	 Â the	 Â two	 Â official	 Â series	 Â for	 Â the	 Â four	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â

                             Wu,	 Â Deng,	 Â and	 Â Liu	 Â (2014)	 Â have	 Â made	 Â a	 Â notable	 Â attempt	 Â to	 Â construct	 Â micro-Â­â€based,	 Â constant	 Â

	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
12
  	 Â The	 Â details	 Â of	 Â the	 Â statistical	 Â procedure	 Â used	 Â in	 Â the	 Â â€œNBS	 Â 70-Â­â€city	 Â Indexâ€	 Â can	 Â be	 Â found	 Â at	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         21	 Â 
	 Â 
Market	 Â Information	 Â System"	 Â (REMIS)	 Â maintained	 Â by	 Â municipal	 Â housing	 Â authorities.	 Â This	 Â data	 Â set	 Â

D. Experiences	 Â in	 Â Japan	 Â and	 Â Singapore	 Â

13

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         22	 Â 
	 Â 
the	 Â past	 Â 25	 Â years.	 Â During	 Â this	 Â period,	 Â the	 Â urban	 Â land	 Â price	 Â index	 Â continued	 Â to	 Â fall	 Â by	 Â half	 Â and	 Â

       Panel	 Â B	 Â of	 Â Figure	 Â 9	 Â depicts	 Â the	 Â private	 Â property	 Â resale	 Â price	 Â index	 Â for	 Â Singapore,	 Â which	 Â is	 Â

                                                                 4. Mortgage	 Â Borrowers	 Â 

       It	 Â is	 Â possible	 Â to	 Â explain	 Â the	 Â dramatic	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â appreciation	 Â in	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â by	 Â the	 Â

                                                                                                                                                         23	 Â 
	 Â 
supply	 Â is	 Â not	 Â the	 Â only	 Â factor	 Â that	 Â matters	 Â to	 Â housing	 Â markets.	 Â As	 Â the	 Â rising	 Â housing	 Â prices	 Â

burdens	 Â faced	 Â by	 Â home	 Â buyers,	 Â especially	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â home	 Â buyers.	 Â 

       Our	 Â detailed	 Â mortgage	 Â data	 Â allow	 Â us	 Â to	 Â provide	 Â a	 Â comprehensive	 Â picture	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â

the	 Â financial	 Â burdens	 Â faced	 Â by	 Â these	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers.	 Â 

       Note	 Â that	 Â households	 Â in	 Â the	 Â most	 Â wealthy	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â population	 Â may	 Â purchase	 Â homes	 Â 
using	 Â cash	 Â and	 Â thus	 Â do	 Â not	 Â appear	 Â in	 Â our	 Â mortgage	 Â data.	 Â For	 Â this	 Â reason,	 Â our	 Â mortgage	 Â data	 Â is	 Â 
particularly	 Â useful	 Â for	 Â analyzing	 Â the	 Â characteristics	 Â of	 Â relatively	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â home	 Â buyers	 Â as	 Â 
opposed	 Â to	 Â that	 Â of	 Â high-Â­â€income	 Â buyers.	 Â We	 Â focus	 Â on	 Â analyzing	 Â two	 Â sets	 Â of	 Â borrowers	 Â in	 Â each	 Â 
tier	 Â of	 Â cities:	 Â The	 Â first	 Â set	 Â has	 Â household	 Â income	 Â in	 Â the	 Â bottom	 Â 10%	 Â among	 Â all	 Â mortgage	 Â 
borrowers	 Â in	 Â a	 Â given	 Â city	 Â and	 Â a	 Â given	 Â year.	 Â We	 Â refer	 Â to	 Â this	 Â set	 Â as	 Â the	 Â bottom-Â­â€income	 Â borrower	 Â 
group.	 Â We	 Â also	 Â denote	 Â borrowers	 Â with	 Â income	 Â exactly	 Â at	 Â the	 Â 10	 Â percentile	 Â of	 Â all	 Â borrowers	 Â by	 Â 
p10.	 Â The	 Â second	 Â set	 Â has	 Â household	 Â income	 Â in	 Â the	 Â middle	 Â range,	 Â specifically	 Â within	 Â the	 Â 45th	 Â and	 Â 
55th	 Â percentile	 Â of	 Â all	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â in	 Â a	 Â given	 Â city	 Â and	 Â a	 Â given	 Â year.	 Â We	 Â refer	 Â to	 Â this	 Â set	 Â 
the	 Â middle-Â­â€income	 Â group	 Â and	 Â denote	 Â borrowers	 Â with	 Â exactly	 Â the	 Â median	 Â income	 Â of	 Â all	 Â 
borrowers	 Â by	 Â p50.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

A. Household	 Â Income	 Â 

                                                                  [Figure	 Â 10	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

       Figure	 Â 10	 Â depicts	 Â the	 Â time-Â­â€series	 Â of	 Â the	 Â household	 Â income	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â and	 Â p50	 Â for	 Â first-Â­â€,	 Â second-Â­â€
and	 Â third-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â in	 Â Panels	 Â A,	 Â B,	 Â and	 Â C,	 Â respectively.	 Â In	 Â panels	 Â A	 Â and	 Â B,	 Â the	 Â left	 Â plot	 Â shows	 Â the	 Â 
annual	 Â income	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â and	 Â p50	 Â (which	 Â is	 Â averaged	 Â across	 Â all	 Â cities	 Â in	 Â the	 Â tier)	 Â in	 Â RMB	 Â from	 Â 2003-Â­â€
2012,	 Â and	 Â the	 Â right	 Â plot	 Â shows	 Â the	 Â position	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â and	 Â p50	 Â in	 Â the	 Â income	 Â distribution	 Â of	 Â the	 Â city	 Â 
population	 Â based	 Â on	 Â the	 Â income	 Â distribution	 Â constructed	 Â from	 Â the	 Â Urban	 Â Household	 Â Survey	 Â 



                                                                                                                                                                 24	 Â 
	 Â 
(UHS).	 Â As	 Â income	 Â distribution	 Â is	 Â not	 Â available	 Â for	 Â third-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â Panel	 Â C	 Â shows	 Â only	 Â the	 Â annual	 Â 
income	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â and	 Â p50.	 Â 	 Â 

       Figure	 Â 10	 Â shows	 Â steady	 Â growth	 Â in	 Â the	 Â household	 Â income	 Â of	 Â both	 Â p10	 Â and	 Â p50	 Â across	 Â the	 Â 
three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â cities.	 Â In	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â the	 Â annual	 Â household	 Â income	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â grew	 Â from	 Â 39,000	 Â 
RMB	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â 92,000	 Â in	 Â 2012	 Â while	 Â the	 Â income	 Â of	 Â p50	 Â grew	 Â from	 Â 87,000	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â 184,000	 Â in	 Â 
2012.	 Â In	 Â second-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â the	 Â annual	 Â income	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â grew	 Â from	 Â 19,000	 Â RMB	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â 58,000	 Â 
in	 Â 2012,	 Â while	 Â that	 Â of	 Â p50	 Â from	 Â 40,000	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â 99,000	 Â in	 Â 2012.	 Â In	 Â third-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â the	 Â income	 Â 
of	 Â p10	 Â grew	 Â from	 Â 15,000	 Â to	 Â 51,000,	 Â while	 Â that	 Â of	 Â p50	 Â grew	 Â from	 Â 28,000	 Â to	 Â 83,000.	 Â This	 Â 
tremendous	 Â income	 Â growth	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â is	 Â largely	 Â consistent	 Â with	 Â the	 Â income	 Â 
growth	 Â of	 Â the	 Â overall	 Â urban	 Â population	 Â we	 Â discussed	 Â above.	 Â 

       For	 Â most	 Â of	 Â the	 Â first-Â­â€	 Â and	 Â second-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â the	 Â UHS	 Â provides	 Â income	 Â distribution	 Â of	 Â urban	 Â 
households.	 Â To	 Â specifically	 Â compare	 Â the	 Â income	 Â growth	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â with	 Â that	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 
urban	 Â population,	 Â we	 Â mark	 Â the	 Â position	 Â of	 Â p10	 Â and	 Â p50	 Â in	 Â the	 Â population	 Â income	 Â distribution	 Â 
reported	 Â by	 Â the	 Â UHS.	 Â As	 Â our	 Â data	 Â from	 Â the	 Â UHS	 Â cover	 Â only	 Â 2003-Â­â€2009,	 Â we	 Â extrapolate	 Â the	 Â 
income	 Â distribution	 Â in	 Â 2009	 Â into	 Â the	 Â subsequent	 Â years	 Â based	 Â on	 Â the	 Â cityâ€™s	 Â average	 Â income	 Â 
growth.	 Â 	 Â 

       The	 Â median-Â­â€income	 Â borrower	 Â p50	 Â came	 Â from	 Â the	 Â relatively	 Â wealthy	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 
population.	 Â In	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â p50	 Â declined	 Â from	 Â the	 Â 85th	 Â percentile	 Â of	 Â the	 Â population	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â 
to	 Â the	 Â 59th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2009	 Â and	 Â then	 Â climbed	 Â back	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 75th	 Â percentile.	 Â In	 Â second-Â­â€tier	 Â 
cities,	 Â p50	 Â declined	 Â from	 Â the	 Â 81.5th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 62th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2010	 Â and	 Â then	 Â 
climbed	 Â back	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 68th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2012.	 Â 	 Â 

       The	 Â position	 Â of	 Â the	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â mortgage	 Â borrower	 Â p10	 Â is	 Â particularly	 Â interesting.	 Â It	 Â 
indicates	 Â the	 Â extent	 Â to	 Â which	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â households	 Â in	 Â the	 Â population	 Â were	 Â participating	 Â in	 Â 
the	 Â housing	 Â markets.	 Â Overall,	 Â p10	 Â was	 Â located	 Â at	 Â a	 Â position	 Â around	 Â the	 Â 25th	 Â percentile	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 
population	 Â in	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â and	 Â around	 Â the	 Â 30th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â second-Â­â€tier	 Â cities.	 Â These	 Â 
positions	 Â indicate	 Â that	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â were	 Â not	 Â just	 Â coming	 Â from	 Â the	 Â top-Â­â€income	 Â 
households	 Â and	 Â instead	 Â were	 Â reasonably	 Â well	 Â represented	 Â in	 Â the	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 
population.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                           25	 Â 
	 Â 
        Interestingly,	 Â despite	 Â the	 Â rapid	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â appreciation	 Â in	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â p10	 Â steadily	 Â 
declined	 Â from	 Â a	 Â position	 Â around	 Â the	 Â 35th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2003	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 17.5th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2010	 Â 
before	 Â it	 Â climbed	 Â back	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 26th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2012.	 Â This	 Â suggests	 Â that	 Â the	 Â rapidly	 Â growing	 Â 
prices	 Â in	 Â recent	 Â years	 Â have	 Â not	 Â prevented	 Â households	 Â from	 Â the	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 
population	 Â from	 Â buying	 Â homes.	 Â In	 Â second-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â p10	 Â stayed	 Â in	 Â a	 Â range	 Â between	 Â the	 Â 28th	 Â 
and	 Â 40th	 Â percentile-Â­â€-Â­â€-Â­â€it	 Â declined	 Â from	 Â a	 Â peak	 Â of	 Â the	 Â 40th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2005	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 28.5th	 Â 
percentile	 Â in	 Â 2010	 Â and	 Â then	 Â climbed	 Â back	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 35th	 Â percentile	 Â in	 Â 2012.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

        Taken	 Â together,	 Â Figure	 Â 10	 Â shows	 Â steady	 Â increases	 Â in	 Â the	 Â household	 Â income	 Â of	 Â bottom-Â­â€	 Â and	 Â 
middle-Â­â€income	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â across	 Â the	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â cities.	 Â 	 Â Furthermore,	 Â despite	 Â the	 Â 
tremendous	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â appreciation	 Â in	 Â these	 Â cities,	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â were	 Â well	 Â 
represented	 Â in	 Â the	 Â population	 Â and	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â participation	 Â of	 Â households	 Â from	 Â the	 Â 
low-Â­â€income	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â population	 Â remained	 Â stable.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

B. Down	 Â Payment	 Â 

        Mortgage	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â is	 Â a	 Â key	 Â variable	 Â that	 Â determines	 Â the	 Â leverage	 Â used	 Â by	 Â mortgage	 Â 
borrowers	 Â and	 Â serves	 Â as	 Â an	 Â equity	 Â buffer	 Â to	 Â prevent	 Â borrowers	 Â from	 Â defaulting	 Â on	 Â the	 Â loans	 Â in	 Â 
the	 Â event	 Â of	 Â a	 Â future	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â meltdown.	 Â Figure	 Â 11	 Â depicts	 Â the	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â 
in	 Â the	 Â home	 Â value	 Â at	 Â the	 Â time	 Â of	 Â purchase,	 Â separately	 Â for	 Â the	 Â bottom-Â­â€	 Â and	 Â middle-Â­â€income	 Â 
groups.	 Â 	 Â 

                                                              [Figure	 Â 11	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

        The	 Â right	 Â panel	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â for	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â in	 Â the	 Â middle-Â­â€income	 Â group,	 Â down	 Â 
payment	 Â on	 Â average	 Â contributed	 Â to	 Â at	 Â least	 Â 35%	 Â of	 Â home	 Â value	 Â across	 Â the	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â cities.	 Â 
Interestingly,	 Â the	 Â left	 Â panel	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â for	 Â borrowers	 Â in	 Â the	 Â bottom-Â­â€income	 Â group,	 Â the	 Â fraction	 Â 
of	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â was	 Â even	 Â higher-Â­â€-Â­â€-Â­â€it	 Â was	 Â consistently	 Â above	 Â 38%	 Â across	 Â the	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â 
cities.	 Â 	 Â 

        These	 Â high	 Â levels	 Â of	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â are	 Â consistent	 Â with	 Â the	 Â strict	 Â mortgage	 Â policies	 Â imposed	 Â 
by	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â government	 Â on	 Â banks.	 Â Specifically,	 Â the	 Â policies	 Â restrict	 Â one	 Â housing	 Â unit	 Â from	 Â 
being	 Â used	 Â as	 Â collateral	 Â for	 Â more	 Â than	 Â one	 Â mortgage	 Â loan.	 Â The	 Â policies	 Â also	 Â require	 Â a	 Â minimum	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                    26	 Â 
	 Â 
down	 Â payment	 Â of	 Â 30%	 Â on	 Â first	 Â mortgages.	 Â As	 Â detailed	 Â in	 Â Appendix	 Â A,	 Â this	 Â minimum	 Â down	 Â 
payment	 Â requirement	 Â had	 Â changed	 Â over	 Â time	 Â between	 Â two	 Â levels:	 Â 30%	 Â or	 Â 40%.	 Â Banks	 Â have	 Â 
requested	 Â even	 Â higher	 Â down	 Â payments	 Â on	 Â second	 Â mortgages	 Â that	 Â are	 Â used	 Â to	 Â finance	 Â 
purchases	 Â of	 Â second	 Â homes.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

                             The	 Â high	 Â levels	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â used	 Â by	 Â Chinese	 Â borrowers	 Â were	 Â in	 Â sharp	 Â 
contrast	 Â to	 Â the	 Â popular	 Â use	 Â of	 Â zero	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â loans	 Â and	 Â negative	 Â amortization	 Â loans	 Â during	 Â 
the	 Â U.S.	 Â housing	 Â bubble	 Â of	 Â 2000s.	 Â According	 Â to	 Â Mayer,	 Â Pence,	 Â and	 Â Sherlund	 Â (2009),	 Â during	 Â the	 Â 
U.S.	 Â housing	 Â bubble	 Â period	 Â of	 Â 2003-Â­â€2006,	 Â households	 Â with	 Â poor	 Â credit	 Â (the	 Â so-Â­â€called	 Â subprime	 Â 
and	 Â Alt-Â­â€A	 Â households)	 Â had	 Â commonly	 Â used	 Â mortgages	 Â with	 Â a	 Â 5%	 Â or	 Â zero	 Â down	 Â payments	 Â to	 Â 
finance	 Â their	 Â home	 Â purchases.	 Â Some	 Â mortgages	 Â even	 Â allowed	 Â the	 Â borrowers	 Â to	 Â have	 Â negative	 Â 
amortization	 Â over	 Â time.	 Â When	 Â the	 Â U.S.	 Â housing	 Â prices	 Â started	 Â to	 Â decline	 Â after	 Â 2006,	 Â these	 Â 
borrowers	 Â were	 Â more	 Â likely	 Â to	 Â default	 Â on	 Â their	 Â mortgage	 Â loans,	 Â exacerbating	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â 
market	 Â decline.	 Â The	 Â high	 Â levels	 Â of	 Â down	 Â payments	 Â used	 Â by	 Â households	 Â throughout	 Â China	 Â 
mitigated	 Â the	 Â risk	 Â of	 Â household	 Â default	 Â in	 Â the	 Â event	 Â of	 Â a	 Â future	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â meltdown.	 Â 
Unless	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â prices	 Â decline	 Â by	 Â over	 Â 30%,	 Â the	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â are	 Â unlikely	 Â to	 Â default	 Â 
on	 Â their	 Â loans.	 Â 14	 Â Furthermore,	 Â mortgage	 Â loans	 Â in	 Â China	 Â are	 Â all	 Â recourse	 Â loans,	 Â which	 Â allow	 Â 
lenders	 Â to	 Â collect	 Â borrowersâ€™	 Â other	 Â assets	 Â in	 Â the	 Â event	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â defaults.	 Â These	 Â reasons	 Â 
make	 Â a	 Â U.S.	 Â style	 Â subprime	 Â credit	 Â crisis	 Â less	 Â of	 Â a	 Â concern	 Â for	 Â China.	 Â 

C. Price-Â­â€to-Â­â€Income	 Â Ratio	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    [Figure	 Â 12	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

                             Price-Â­â€to-Â­â€income	 Â ratio	 Â provides	 Â a	 Â convenient	 Â measure	 Â of	 Â the	 Â financial	 Â burdens	 Â endured	 Â by	 Â a	 Â

panel).	 Â In	 Â each	 Â panel,	 Â there	 Â are	 Â two	 Â plots,	 Â the	 Â left	 Â plot	 Â covers	 Â the	 Â borrowers	 Â in	 Â the	 Â bottom-Â­â€



	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
14
  	 Â According	 Â to	 Â annual	 Â reports	 Â of	 Â the	 Â China	 Â Banking	 Â Regulatory	 Â Commission	 Â (CBRC),	 Â the	 Â ratio	 Â of	 Â non-Â­â€performing	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          27	 Â

income	 Â group	 Â with	 Â a	 Â separate	 Â line	 Â for	 Â each	 Â of	 Â the	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â cities,	 Â while	 Â the	 Â right	 Â plot	 Â covers	 Â

                                                                  [Figure	 Â 13	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

       Home	 Â size	 Â is	 Â an	 Â important	 Â dimension	 Â for	 Â determining	 Â the	 Â consumption	 Â value	 Â of	 Â a	 Â home.	 Â 
Figure	 Â 13	 Â depicts	 Â the	 Â size	 Â of	 Â the	 Â homes	 Â purchased	 Â by	 Â bottom-Â­â€income	 Â and	 Â middle-Â­â€income	 Â

72	 Â and	 Â 80	 Â square	 Â meters	 Â throughout	 Â the	 Â decade.	 Â For	 Â a	 Â typical	 Â family	 Â of	 Â three	 Â (a	 Â couple	 Â with	 Â 	 Â

       It	 Â is	 Â also	 Â useful	 Â to	 Â note	 Â the	 Â evident	 Â declining	 Â trend	 Â in	 Â the	 Â home	 Â size	 Â purchased	 Â by	 Â all	 Â groups	 Â

E. Age	 Â and	 Â Marital	 Â Status	 Â 

                                                                  [Figure	 Â 14	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

       Figure	 Â 14	 Â shows	 Â the	 Â age	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers	 Â in	 Â our	 Â sample.	 Â Across	 Â the	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â

                                                                                                                                                         31	 Â 
	 Â 
                             A	 Â significant	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â borrowers	 Â are	 Â unmarried.	 Â Figure	 Â 15	 Â summarizes	 Â the	 Â fractions	 Â of	 Â

                             Wei,	 Â Zhang,	 Â and	 Â Liu	 Â (2014)	 Â argue	 Â that	 Â home	 Â ownership	 Â is	 Â a	 Â status	 Â good	 Â for	 Â single	 Â men	 Â to	 Â

F. Second	 Â Mortgages	 Â 	 Â 

                             An	 Â often	 Â discussed	 Â concern	 Â regarding	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â markets	 Â is	 Â that	 Â many	 Â urban	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    [Table	 Â 2	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 
	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
17
  	 Â This	 Â pattern	 Â is	 Â consistent	 Â with	 Â casual	 Â observations	 Â that	 Â sex	 Â imbalance	 Â is	 Â less	 Â severe	 Â in	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â as	 Â people	 Â in	 Â

  	 Â As	 Â we	 Â explain	 Â in	 Â Section	 Â 6,	 Â in	 Â early	 Â 2010	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â government	 Â raised	 Â the	 Â down-Â­â€payment	 Â ratio	 Â and	 Â the	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        32	 Â 
	 Â 
                             Table	 Â 2	 Â summarizes	 Â the	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â second	 Â mortgages	 Â among	 Â all	 Â mortgage	 Â loans	 Â issued	 Â in	 Â

                             In	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities,	 Â the	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â second	 Â mortgages	 Â was	 Â 5.3%	 Â and	 Â 5.2%	 Â in	 Â 2011	 Â and	 Â 2012,	 Â

                             In	 Â summary,	 Â our	 Â analysis	 Â of	 Â the	 Â mortgage	 Â data	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â despite	 Â the	 Â enormous	 Â housing	 Â

and	 Â further	 Â high	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â appreciation.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 


                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        5. Housing	 Â as	 Â an	 Â Investment	 Â Vehicle	 Â 

                             The	 Â spectacular	 Â economic	 Â growth	 Â of	 Â China	 Â since	 Â the	 Â 1980s	 Â has	 Â been	 Â accompanied	 Â by	 Â a	 Â high	 Â 
savings	 Â rate.	 Â According	 Â to	 Â Yang,	 Â Zhang,	 Â and	 Â Zhou	 Â (2013),	 Â the	 Â gross	 Â national	 Â savings	 Â as	 Â a	 Â

	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
19
  	 Â The	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â second	 Â mortgages	 Â in	 Â our	 Â mortgage	 Â data	 Â may	 Â appear	 Â substantially	 Â lower	 Â than	 Â the	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â 
households	 Â owning	 Â multiple	 Â homes	 Â reported	 Â by	 Â Chinese	 Â Household	 Â Finance	 Â Survey	 Â (2012).	 Â According	 Â to	 Â this	 Â 
survey	 Â conducted	 Â in	 Â 2012,	 Â the	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â urban	 Â households	 Â owning	 Â at	 Â least	 Â two	 Â apartments	 Â in	 Â China	 Â is	 Â 15.44%.	 Â 
Note	 Â that	 Â a	 Â significant	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â the	 Â multiple	 Â homes	 Â owned	 Â by	 Â the	 Â surveyed	 Â households	 Â was	 Â probably	 Â old-Â­â€style	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              33	 Â 
	 Â 
50%	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 2000s.	 Â This	 Â high	 Â saving	 Â rate	 Â surpassed	 Â the	 Â rates	 Â of	 Â all	 Â major	 Â countries	 Â during	 Â the	 Â 
same	 Â period	 Â and	 Â was	 Â also	 Â higher	 Â than	 Â the	 Â prevailing	 Â rates	 Â in	 Â Japan,	 Â South	 Â Korea,	 Â and	 Â other	 Â East	 Â

government	 Â have	 Â all	 Â contributed	 Â to	 Â the	 Â remarkably	 Â high	 Â savings	 Â rate	 Â in	 Â China.	 Â 	 Â Savings	 Â by	 Â 
households	 Â and	 Â firms	 Â had	 Â each	 Â reached	 Â about	 Â 20%	 Â of	 Â the	 Â GDP	 Â during	 Â the	 Â 2000s.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

                             Despite	 Â the	 Â high	 Â savings	 Â rate,	 Â households	 Â and	 Â firms	 Â in	 Â China	 Â have	 Â limited	 Â vehicles	 Â in	 Â which	 Â 
to	 Â invest	 Â their	 Â massive	 Â savings.	 Â Bank	 Â deposit	 Â accounts	 Â are	 Â the	 Â predominant	 Â investment	 Â vehicle	 Â 
in	 Â China.	 Â Due	 Â to	 Â China's	 Â restrictive	 Â capital	 Â controls,	 Â households	 Â and	 Â firms	 Â cannot	 Â freely	 Â invest	 Â

market	 Â inside	 Â China,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â still	 Â small	 Â by	 Â size	 Â relative	 Â to	 Â the	 Â pool	 Â of	 Â savings	 Â and	 Â has	 Â not	 Â offered	 Â 
attractive	 Â returns	 Â in	 Â the	 Â last	 Â two	 Â decades.20	 Â Bond	 Â markets	 Â in	 Â China	 Â are	 Â even	 Â smaller.	 Â Given	 Â 
these	 Â limited	 Â investment	 Â choices,	 Â Chinese	 Â households	 Â often	 Â use	 Â housing	 Â as	 Â an	 Â alternative	 Â 
investment	 Â vehicle.	 Â In	 Â this	 Â section,	 Â we	 Â discuss	 Â the	 Â performance	 Â of	 Â housing	 Â as	 Â an	 Â investment	 Â 
vehicle,	 Â in	 Â comparison	 Â to	 Â bank	 Â deposits	 Â and	 Â stock	 Â market.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

A.	 Â Bank	 Â Deposits	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    [Figure	 Â 16	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

                             Figure	 Â 16	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â total	 Â bank	 Â deposits	 Â in	 Â China	 Â rose	 Â from	 Â slightly	 Â above	 Â 20	 Â trillion	 Â RMB	 Â in	 Â 
2003	 Â to	 Â over	 Â 100	 Â trillion	 Â in	 Â 2013.	 Â 	 Â The	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â is	 Â regulated	 Â by	 Â the	 Â central	 Â bank.	 Â As	 Â shown	 Â in	 Â 
Figure	 Â 4,	 Â the	 Â nominal	 Â bank	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â remained	 Â in	 Â a	 Â narrow	 Â range,	 Â between	 Â 2	 Â and	 Â 4	 Â percent	 Â 
in	 Â 2003-Â­â€2013,	 Â while	 Â the	 Â national	 Â inflation	 Â rate	 Â frequently	 Â surpassed	 Â the	 Â nominal	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â in	 Â 
2004,	 Â 2008-Â­â€2009,	 Â and	 Â 2011-Â­â€2012,	 Â making	 Â the	 Â real	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â negative.	 Â The	 Â average	 Â real	 Â

deposits	 Â rather	 Â striking.	 Â Nevertheless,	 Â the	 Â low	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â has	 Â motivated	 Â Chinese	 Â households	 Â 
to	 Â search	 Â for	 Â alternative	 Â vehicles	 Â to	 Â invest	 Â their	 Â massive	 Â savings.	 Â 	 Â 


	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 
20
  	 Â There	 Â is	 Â a	 Â debate	 Â regarding	 Â the	 Â performance	 Â of	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â stock	 Â market.	 Â On	 Â one	 Â hand,	 Â Allen,	 Â et	 Â al	 Â (2014)	 Â argue	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          34	 Â 
	 Â 
B.	 Â Stocks	 Â 

       China	 Â established	 Â two	 Â stock	 Â exchanges	 Â in	 Â the	 Â early	 Â 1990s,	 Â one	 Â in	 Â Shanghai	 Â and	 Â the	 Â other	 Â in	 Â

deposits.	 Â 	 Â

       Overall,	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â returns	 Â from	 Â all	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â Chinese	 Â cities	 Â were	 Â surprisingly	 Â resilient	 Â

                                                                6. 	 Â Discussion	 Â 

       Our	 Â analysis	 Â provides	 Â some	 Â basic	 Â facts	 Â to	 Â allow	 Â us	 Â to	 Â understand	 Â and	 Â interpret	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â 
housing	 Â boom.	 Â In	 Â this	 Â section,	 Â we	 Â offer	 Â discussion	 Â based	 Â on	 Â these	 Â facts.	 Â 

       Housing	 Â booms	 Â are	 Â often	 Â associated	 Â with	 Â credit	 Â expansions,	 Â with	 Â the	 Â recent	 Â housing	 Â bubble	 Â 
in	 Â the	 Â U.S.	 Â being	 Â a	 Â vivid	 Â example.	 Â As	 Â a	 Â result,	 Â commentators	 Â have	 Â often	 Â been	 Â concerned	 Â by	 Â the	 Â 
potential	 Â risk	 Â of	 Â excessive	 Â leverage	 Â in	 Â driving	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â boom.	 Â As	 Â we	 Â have	 Â described	 Â 
earlier,	 Â Chinese	 Â banks	 Â have	 Â required	 Â down	 Â payments	 Â of	 Â over	 Â 30	 Â percent	 Â for	 Â all	 Â mortgage	 Â loans.	 Â 
Such	 Â high	 Â down	 Â payment	 Â ratios	 Â substantially	 Â alleviate	 Â the	 Â household	 Â default	 Â risk	 Â faced	 Â by	 Â banks	 Â 
and	 Â make	 Â reckless	 Â credit	 Â expansions	 Â to	 Â households	 Â an	 Â unlikely	 Â cause	 Â of	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â 
boom.	 Â 	 Â 




                                                                                                                                                    36	 Â 
	 Â 
       A	 Â particular	 Â concern	 Â about	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â boom	 Â is	 Â that	 Â the	 Â enormous	 Â housing	 Â price	 Â 
appreciation	 Â across	 Â Chinese	 Â cities	 Â might	 Â constitute	 Â a	 Â housing	 Â bubble	 Â that	 Â is	 Â about	 Â to	 Â burst.	 Â As	 Â is	 Â 
well	 Â known	 Â by	 Â economists,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â difficult	 Â to	 Â reliably	 Â identify	 Â an	 Â asset	 Â bubble.	 Â There	 Â may	 Â or	 Â may	 Â 
not	 Â be	 Â a	 Â housing	 Â bubble	 Â in	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â market.	 Â To	 Â forcefully	 Â determine	 Â the	 Â presence	 Â of	 Â 
a	 Â housing	 Â bubble,	 Â one	 Â needs	 Â to	 Â develop	 Â a	 Â systematic	 Â framework	 Â of	 Â supply	 Â and	 Â demand	 Â of	 Â 
housing	 Â that	 Â fully	 Â accounts	 Â for	 Â the	 Â growth	 Â of	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â economy	 Â and	 Â the	 Â frictions	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 
Chinese	 Â financial	 Â system.	 Â This	 Â entails	 Â a	 Â task	 Â substantially	 Â more	 Â ambitious	 Â than	 Â the	 Â goal	 Â of	 Â this	 Â 
paper.	 Â Nevertheless,	 Â our	 Â analysis	 Â offers	 Â some	 Â useful	 Â observations	 Â about	 Â the	 Â nature	 Â of	 Â this	 Â 
nationwide	 Â housing	 Â boom.	 Â 

       On	 Â one	 Â hand,	 Â the	 Â enormous	 Â price	 Â appreciation	 Â does	 Â not	 Â necessarily	 Â indicate	 Â the	 Â presence	 Â 
of	 Â a	 Â bubble,	 Â as	 Â the	 Â price	 Â appreciation	 Â has	 Â been	 Â mostly	 Â accompanied	 Â by	 Â equally	 Â impressive	 Â 
household	 Â income	 Â growth,	 Â except	 Â in	 Â a	 Â few	 Â first-Â­â€tier	 Â cities.	 Â 	 Â On	 Â the	 Â other	 Â hand,	 Â housing	 Â prices	 Â 
are	 Â indeed	 Â expensive	 Â relative	 Â to	 Â the	 Â income	 Â of	 Â many	 Â households,	 Â in	 Â particular,	 Â of	 Â the	 Â low-Â­â€
income	 Â mortgage	 Â borrowers.	 Â While	 Â one	 Â can	 Â justify	 Â their	 Â willingness,	 Â despite	 Â the	 Â severe	 Â 
financial	 Â burdens,	 Â to	 Â buy	 Â homes	 Â based	 Â on	 Â expectations	 Â of	 Â persistently	 Â high	 Â income	 Â growth,	 Â the	 Â 
high	 Â price-Â­â€to-Â­â€income	 Â ratios	 Â observed	 Â across	 Â Chinese	 Â cities	 Â expose	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â to	 Â 
substantial	 Â risks	 Â when	 Â householdsâ€™	 Â expectations	 Â are	 Â subdued	 Â in	 Â the	 Â future,	 Â especially	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 
event	 Â of	 Â a	 Â sudden	 Â stop	 Â in	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â economy.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

       Our	 Â analysis	 Â also	 Â highlights	 Â a	 Â few	 Â key	 Â ingredients	 Â for	 Â future	 Â efforts	 Â to	 Â develop	 Â a	 Â systematic	 Â 
framework	 Â for	 Â analyzing	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â market:	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

       1. The	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â across	 Â first-Â­â€,	 Â second-Â­â€,	 Â and	 Â third-Â­â€tier	 Â cities	 Â offered	 Â high	 Â returns,	 Â 
             substantially	 Â more	 Â attractive	 Â than	 Â bank	 Â deposits	 Â and	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â stock	 Â market.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

       2. Housing	 Â price	 Â levels	 Â were	 Â also	 Â high	 Â across	 Â the	 Â three	 Â tiers	 Â of	 Â cities	 Â with	 Â low-Â­â€income	 Â 
             households	 Â buying	 Â homes	 Â at	 Â prices	 Â that	 Â were	 Â over	 Â eight	 Â times	 Â their	 Â annual	 Â income.	 Â 	 Â 

       3. Concurrent	 Â with	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â boom,	 Â Chinese	 Â households	 Â also	 Â accumulated	 Â a	 Â large	 Â pool	 Â 
             of	 Â cash	 Â in	 Â bank	 Â deposits	 Â that	 Â paid	 Â a	 Â minimal	 Â deposit	 Â rate.	 Â 	 Â 



                                                                                                                                                    37	 Â 
	 Â 
                             It	 Â is	 Â important	 Â to	 Â fully	 Â account	 Â for	 Â the	 Â distortions	 Â brought	 Â to	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â by	 Â the	 Â 
imperfections	 Â in	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â financial	 Â system.	 Â The	 Â low	 Â bank	 Â deposit	 Â rate	 Â that	 Â had	 Â persisted	 Â 
throughout	 Â the	 Â studied	 Â decade	 Â is	 Â a	 Â key	 Â factor.	 Â To	 Â the	 Â extent	 Â that	 Â near	 Â 100	 Â trillion	 Â RMB	 Â has	 Â 
been	 Â sitting	 Â in	 Â bank	 Â deposits	 Â earning	 Â an	 Â almost	 Â zero	 Â real	 Â rate,	 Â householdsâ€™	 Â investment	 Â demand	 Â 
for	 Â housing	 Â is	 Â an	 Â important	 Â driver	 Â of	 Â housing	 Â prices	 Â in	 Â China.21	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

                             Beside	 Â the	 Â well-Â­â€known	 Â illiquidity	 Â of	 Â housing,	 Â risk	 Â is	 Â an	 Â important	 Â dimension	 Â in	 Â evaluating	 Â 
housing	 Â as	 Â an	 Â investment	 Â asset.	 Â It	 Â is	 Â difficult	 Â to	 Â measure	 Â the	 Â crash	 Â risk	 Â of	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â

21

                                       7. Roles	 Â of	 Â Government	 Â in	 Â the	 Â Housing	 Â Market	 Â

A. Interventions	 Â by	 Â the	 Â Central	 Â Government	 Â

23

                             The	 Â frequent	 Â interventions	 Â by	 Â the	 Â central	 Â government	 Â have	 Â created	 Â a	 Â sense	 Â among	 Â the	 Â

B. Land	 Â Sales	 Â and	 Â Fiscal	 Â Revenue	 Â of	 Â Local	 Â Governments	 Â 

                             Land	 Â sale	 Â revenues	 Â have	 Â contributed	 Â to	 Â a	 Â substantial	 Â fraction	 Â of	 Â local	 Â governmentsâ€™	 Â budgets.	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  [Figure	 Â 18	 Â about	 Â Here]	 Â 

                             As	 Â a	 Â result	 Â of	 Â this	 Â central/local	 Â fiscal	 Â arrangement	 Â and	 Â the	 Â restrictions	 Â placed	 Â on	 Â local	 Â

	 Â 
2011	 Â averaged	 Â across	 Â all	 Â cities,	 Â as	 Â well	 Â as	 Â among	 Â the	 Â first-Â­â€tier,	 Â second-Â­â€tier,	 Â and	 Â all	 Â other	 Â cities.	 Â

                             In	 Â this	 Â section,	 Â we	 Â discuss	 Â the	 Â key	 Â risk	 Â factors	 Â facing	 Â the	 Â Chinese	 Â housing	 Â market.	 Â The	 Â most	 Â

25

	 Â

       First,	 Â we	 Â consider	 Â the	 Â possible	 Â imposition	 Â of	 Â property	 Â taxes	 Â and	 Â new	 Â fiscal	 Â reform.	 Â Property	 Â

policymakersâ€™	 Â agendas.	 Â Part	 Â of	 Â the	 Â reason	 Â is	 Â a	 Â desire	 Â to	 Â increase	 Â the	 Â cost	 Â of	 Â speculative	 Â

local	 Â government	 Â budgets	 Â that	 Â have,	 Â as	 Â we	 Â have	 Â shown,	 Â become	 Â increasing	 Â reliant	 Â on	 Â land	 Â sales	 Â 
revenue.	 Â 	 Â 

       An	 Â introduction	 Â of	 Â property	 Â taxes	 Â will	 Â have	 Â several	 Â effects	 Â on	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market.	 Â First,	 Â it	 Â

house,	 Â plus	 Â the	 Â property	 Â tax	 Â assessment.	 Â If	 Â the	 Â property	 Â tax	 Â rate	 Â is	 Â chosen	 Â properly,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â

property	 Â taxes,	 Â in	 Â conjunction	 Â with	 Â fiscal	 Â reform	 Â that	 Â provides	 Â local	 Â governments	 Â with	 Â new	 Â 
revenue	 Â sources	 Â that	 Â are	 Â not	 Â tied	 Â to	 Â selling	 Â land,	 Â is	 Â likely	 Â to	 Â affect	 Â householdsâ€™	 Â expectations	 Â that	 Â 
the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â is	 Â â€œtoo	 Â important	 Â to	 Â fall.â€	 Â Absent	 Â such	 Â a	 Â conviction,	 Â the	 Â perceived	 Â risk	 Â

and	 Â other	 Â fiscal	 Â reform	 Â will	 Â also	 Â put	 Â downward	 Â pressure	 Â on	 Â the	 Â price	 Â that	 Â developers	 Â are	 Â willing	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                       44	 Â 
	 Â 
to	 Â pay	 Â for	 Â land,	 Â which	 Â in	 Â turn	 Â would	 Â further	 Â lower	 Â the	 Â cost	 Â of	 Â housing.	 Â All	 Â three	 Â effects	 Â of	 Â

       Another	 Â pending	 Â reform	 Â that	 Â may	 Â impact	 Â the	 Â housing	 Â market	 Â is	 Â the	 Â unification	 Â of	 Â the	 Â

China,	 Â particularly	 Â the	 Â health	 Â care	 Â and	 Â health	 Â insurance	 Â segments,	 Â is	 Â poorly	 Â designed	 Â to	 Â

Specifically,	 Â most	 Â of	 Â the	 Â best	 Â hospitals	 Â in	 Â China	 Â are	 Â located	 Â either	 Â in	 Â Beijing	 Â and	 Â Shanghai,	 Â or	 Â in	 Â

(see	 Â Fang,	 Â 2014,	 Â for	 Â an	 Â overview	 Â of	 Â the	 Â social	 Â insurance	 Â arrangement	 Â in	 Â China).	 Â Health	 Â

hospitals,	 Â but	 Â would	 Â also	 Â have	 Â difficulty	 Â in	 Â having	 Â their	 Â medical	 Â expenditures	 Â covered	 Â by	 Â their	 Â

	 Â 
Appendix	 Â A:	 Â Residential	 Â Mortgages	 Â in	 Â China	 Â 

A.1	 Â Borrowing	 Â Requirements	 Â 

   The	 Â borrower	 Â of	 Â a	 Â mortgage	 Â loan	 Â should	 Â have	 Â a	 Â stable	 Â source	 Â of	 Â income	 Â and	 Â a	 Â good	 Â credit	 Â 
record,	 Â and	 Â be	 Â between	 Â 18	 Â to	 Â 65	 Â years	 Â of	 Â age.	 Â Generally,	 Â loan	 Â to	 Â value	 Â ratio	 Â should	 Â be	 Â lower	 Â 
than	 Â  70%,	 Â  and	 Â  the	 Â  loan	 Â  term	 Â  should	 Â  be	 Â  less	 Â  than	 Â  30	 Â  years.	 Â  According	 Â  to	 Â  the	 Â  requirements	 Â 
announced	 Â  by	 Â  the	 Â  China	 Â  Banking	 Â  Regulatory	 Â  Commission	 Â  (CBRC)	 Â  in	 Â  "Guidelines	 Â  for	 Â  the	 Â  risk	 Â

payment	 Â to	 Â income	 Â should	 Â be	 Â lower	 Â than	 Â 55%.	 Â 

        In	 Â China,	 Â  only	 Â  houses	 Â  can	 Â  act	 Â  as	 Â  collateral	 Â  for	 Â  mortgage	 Â loans.	 Â  	 Â  	 Â  This	 Â  includes	 Â  villas,	 Â  with	 Â  the	 Â 
down	 Â payment	 Â  ratio	 Â  of	 Â  a	 Â  villa	 Â  being	 Â  higher	 Â  than	 Â  that	 Â  of	 Â  other	 Â  types	 Â  of	 Â  houses.	 Â  House	 Â  age	 Â  (from	 Â 
its	 Â completion	 Â date)	 Â should	 Â be	 Â no	 Â more	 Â than	 Â 20	 Â to	 Â 30	 Â years,	 Â and	 Â house	 Â age	 Â plus	 Â the	 Â loan	 Â term	 Â 
should	 Â  be	 Â  no	 Â  more	 Â  than	 Â  30	 Â  to	 Â  40	 Â  years.	 Â  Different	 Â  from	 Â  other	 Â  countries,	 Â  such	 Â  as	 Â  the	 Â  U.S.,	 Â 
collateral	 Â of	 Â a	 Â mortgage	 Â loan	 Â has	 Â to	 Â belong	 Â to	 Â the	 Â borrower.	 Â 

A.2	 Â Loan	 Â Application	 Â Procedure	 Â and	 Â Payments	 Â 

      A	 Â mortgage	 Â applicant	 Â should	 Â first	 Â submit	 Â all	 Â required	 Â documents	 Â to	 Â a	 Â bank.	 Â After	 Â receiving	 Â 
the	 Â application,	 Â the	 Â bank	 Â carries	 Â out	 Â an	 Â eligibility	 Â investigation.	 Â The	 Â most	 Â important	 Â factor	 Â for	 Â 
the	 Â investigation	 Â is	 Â the	 Â applicantâ€™s	 Â income	 Â and	 Â credit	 Â record.	 Â The	 Â bank	 Â can	 Â check	 Â an	 Â individualâ€™s	 Â 
credit	 Â record	 Â through	 Â the	 Â credit	 Â rating	 Â system	 Â of	 Â the	 Â People's	 Â Bank	 Â of	 Â China,	 Â which	 Â started	 Â its	 Â 
trial	 Â  operation	 Â  in	 Â  December	 Â  2004	 Â  and	 Â  official	 Â  operation	 Â  in	 Â  January	 Â  2006.	 Â  Upon	 Â  approval,	 Â  the	 Â 
borrower	 Â  signs	 Â  a	 Â  mortgage	 Â  contract	 Â  with	 Â  the	 Â  bank,	 Â  and	 Â  then	 Â  opens	 Â  a	 Â  mortgage	 Â  account	 Â  with	 Â 
the	 Â  bank	 Â  for	 Â  making	 Â  mortgage	 Â  payments.	 Â  If	 Â  the	 Â  loan	 Â  term	 Â  is	 Â  one	 Â  year	 Â  or	 Â  shorter,	 Â  both	 Â  principal	 Â 
and	 Â interest	 Â must	 Â be	 Â repaid	 Â as	 Â a	 Â lump	 Â sum	 Â at	 Â maturity;	 Â if	 Â the	 Â loan	 Â term	 Â is	 Â longer	 Â than	 Â one	 Â year,	 Â 
the	 Â  loan	 Â  may	 Â  be	 Â  repaid	 Â  in	 Â  equal	 Â  installments	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  principal	 Â  plus	 Â  interest,	 Â  or	 Â  in	 Â  equal	 Â 
installments	 Â of	 Â the	 Â principal.	 Â 	 Â 

A.3	 Â Mortgage	 Â Interest	 Â Rate	 Â 

       Interest	 Â rates	 Â for	 Â all	 Â bank	 Â loans,	 Â including	 Â mortgage	 Â loans,	 Â are	 Â regulated	 Â by	 Â the	 Â central	 Â bank	 Â 
of	 Â China,	 Â known	 Â as	 Â the	 Â Peopleâ€™s	 Â Bank	 Â of	 Â China	 Â (PBC).	 Â All	 Â banks	 Â need	 Â to	 Â follow	 Â the	 Â same	 Â lending	 Â

benchmark	 Â  lending	 Â  rates	 Â  it	 Â  sets	 Â  for	 Â  bank	 Â  loans,	 Â  which	 Â  usually	 Â  depend	 Â  on	 Â  loan	 Â  terms.	 Â  This	 Â 
multiple	 Â has	 Â varied	 Â over	 Â time.	 Â It	 Â was	 Â 0.9	 Â before	 Â August	 Â 2006,	 Â 0.85	 Â from	 Â August	 Â 2006	 Â to	 Â October	 Â 
2008,	 Â  and	 Â  0.7	 Â  from	 Â  October	 Â  2008	 Â  to	 Â  March	 Â  2010.	 Â  After	 Â  March	 Â  2010,	 Â  the	 Â  multiple	 Â  for	 Â  a	 Â  first	 Â 

                                                                                                                                                                         46	 Â 
	 Â 
house	 Â was	 Â 0.85,	 Â and	 Â for	 Â a	 Â second	 Â house	 Â was	 Â 1.1.	 Â 	 Â During	 Â our	 Â studied	 Â decade,	 Â the	 Â benchmark	 Â 
lending	 Â rates	 Â for	 Â different	 Â maturities	 Â have	 Â stayed	 Â between	 Â 4	 Â and	 Â 8	 Â percent.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 

    An	 Â  important	 Â  feature	 Â  of	 Â  mortgage	 Â  loans	 Â  in	 Â  China	 Â  is	 Â  that	 Â  all	 Â  are	 Â  adjustable	 Â  rate	 Â  mortgages	 Â 
(ARMs);	 Â  there	 Â  are	 Â  no	 Â  fixed	 Â  rate	 Â  mortgages.26	 Â 	 Â  When	 Â  the	 Â  PBC	 Â  changes	 Â  its	 Â  benchmark	 Â  lending	 Â 
rates,	 Â  interest	 Â  rates	 Â  of	 Â  all	 Â  mortgage	 Â  loans	 Â  are	 Â  adjusted	 Â  according	 Â  to	 Â  the	 Â  most	 Â  recent	 Â  lending	 Â 
rates.	 Â Except	 Â for	 Â a	 Â small	 Â number	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â loans	 Â that	 Â are	 Â adjusted	 Â in	 Â the	 Â next	 Â month	 Â or	 Â next	 Â 
quarter,	 Â the	 Â majority	 Â of	 Â mortgage	 Â loans	 Â are	 Â adjusted	 Â on	 Â the	 Â first	 Â day	 Â of	 Â the	 Â next	 Â year.	 Â 	 Â 

A.4	 Â Mortgage	 Â Termination	 Â 

       Default	 Â and	 Â full	 Â prepayment	 Â are	 Â two	 Â ways	 Â to	 Â terminate	 Â a	 Â mortgage	 Â contract	 Â during	 Â its	 Â term.	 Â 
The	 Â  default	 Â  rate	 Â  in	 Â  China	 Â  has	 Â  been	 Â  low.	 Â  According	 Â  to	 Â  the	 Â  Annual	 Â  Report	 Â  of	 Â  China	 Â  Banking	 Â 
Regulatory	 Â  Commission,27	 Â the	 Â  ratio	 Â  of	 Â  non-Â­â€performing	 Â  loans	 Â  in	 Â  residential	 Â  mortgages	 Â  declined	 Â 
steadily	 Â from	 Â 0.59%	 Â in	 Â 2009	 Â to	 Â 0.26%	 Â in	 Â 2013,	 Â and	 Â the	 Â average	 Â during	 Â this	 Â 5-Â­â€year	 Â period	 Â was	 Â 
0.36%.28	 Â The	 Â  non-Â­â€performing	 Â  ratio	 Â  in	 Â  residential	 Â  mortgage	 Â  loans	 Â  is	 Â  much	 Â  lower	 Â  than	 Â  that	 Â  in	 Â 
credit	 Â  cards,	 Â  which	 Â  was	 Â  on	 Â  average	 Â  1.59%	 Â  during	 Â  the	 Â  same	 Â  period.	 Â  One	 Â  important	 Â  reason	 Â  for	 Â 
the	 Â low	 Â default	 Â rate	 Â in	 Â China	 Â is	 Â that	 Â all	 Â mortgage	 Â loans	 Â are	 Â recourse	 Â loans.	 Â This	 Â allows	 Â mortgage	 Â

a	 Â  default	 Â  occurs	 Â  and	 Â  the	 Â value	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â home	 Â  value	 Â  is	 Â  lower	 Â  than	 Â  the	 Â  loan	 Â balance,	 Â  the	 Â  mortgage	 Â 
lender	 Â can	 Â request	 Â courts	 Â to	 Â sell	 Â the	 Â home	 Â and	 Â collect	 Â the	 Â borrower's	 Â other	 Â assets.	 Â Furthermore,	 Â 
mortgage	 Â refinance,	 Â i.e.,	 Â the	 Â process	 Â of	 Â paying	 Â off	 Â an	 Â existing	 Â loan	 Â by	 Â taking	 Â a	 Â new	 Â loan,	 Â is	 Â not	 Â 
allowed	 Â in	 Â China.29	 Â This	 Â rule	 Â prevents	 Â borrowers	 Â from	 Â using	 Â homes	 Â as	 Â ATMs.	 Â

                                              Figure	 Â 1:	 Â Per	 Â Capita	 Â Area	 Â of	 Â Newly	 Â Built	 Â Residential	 Â Housing	 Â 
                                                                                                     	 Â 
For	 Â each	 Â tier	 Â of	 Â cities,	 Â we	 Â divide	 Â its	 Â annual	 Â flow	 Â of	 Â newly	 Â constructed	 Â residential	 Â housing,	 Â measured	 Â in	 Â square	 Â meters,	 Â 
by	 Â its	 Â urban	 Â population	 Â in	 Â 2012.	 Â The	 Â National	 Â Bureau	 Â of	 Â Statistics	 Â provides	 Â annual	 Â city-Â­â€level	 Â data	 Â on	 Â space	 Â of	 Â newly	 Â 
constructed	 Â residential	 Â housing	 Â from	 Â 2002-Â­â€2013	 Â and	 Â resident	 Â population	 Â from	 Â 2005-Â­â€2012,	 Â for	 Â 35	 Â large	 Â cities	 Â only.	 Â 
These	 Â cities	 Â include	 Â all	 Â four	 Â tier-Â­â€1	 Â cities:	 Â Beijing,	 Â Shanghai,	 Â Guangzhou,	 Â and	 Â Shenzhen.	 Â The	 Â other	 Â 31	 Â cities	 Â all	 Â belong	 Â to	 Â 
the	 Â tier-Â­â€2	 Â cities	 Â defined	 Â in	 Â the	 Â paper.	 Â We	 Â use	 Â the	 Â aggregate	 Â of	 Â these	 Â 31	 Â cities	 Â to	 Â compute	 Â the	 Â per	 Â capita	 Â area	 Â built	 Â for	 Â 
tier-Â­â€2	 Â cities.	 Â 	 Â We	 Â then	 Â subtract	 Â these	 Â 35	 Â cities	 Â from	 Â the	 Â national	 Â aggregates	 Â on	 Â newly	 Â constructed	 Â urban	 Â housing	 Â and	 Â 
urban	 Â population	 Â to	 Â get	 Â measures	 Â for	 Â tier-Â­â€3	 Â (and	 Â other)	 Â cities.	 Â Resident	 Â population	 Â includes	 Â all	 Â people	 Â residing	 Â six	 Â 
months	 Â or	 Â more	 Â in	 Â the	 Â area	 Â governed	 Â by	 Â the	 Â city	 Â in	 Â the	 Â current	 Â year	 Â (in	 Â contrast	 Â to	 Â the	 Â hukou	 Â population).	 Â We	 Â 
assume	 Â all	 Â resident	 Â populations	 Â in	 Â tier-Â­â€1	 Â and	 Â tier-Â­â€2	 Â cities	 Â are	 Â urban,	 Â which	 Â leads	 Â to	 Â a	 Â slight	 Â overestimation	 Â of	 Â urban	 Â 
population	 Â in	 Â tier-Â­â€1	 Â and	 Â tier-Â­â€2	 Â cities	 Â and,	 Â consequently,	 Â a	 Â slight	 Â underestimation	 Â of	 Â urban	 Â population	 Â in	 Â tier-Â­â€3	 Â (and	 Â 
other)	 Â cities.	 Â In	 Â 2012,	 Â China	 Â had	 Â a	 Â total	 Â population	 Â of	 Â 13.5	 Â billion,	 Â out	 Â of	 Â which	 Â 7.2	 Â billion	 Â are	 Â urban	 Â and	 Â 6.3	 Â billion	 Â are	 Â
